		<shortDescription>Tales From The Horror Genre #1</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>With Christmas season in full swing, it seems appropriate to look back at one of the best holiday-themed horror films of all-time. Beginning with the ring of a telephone and ending with that same ring, a sure sign of the effectiveness of "Black Christmas" is revealed when you reflect back upon how this familiar sound changes over its duration. It starts as harmless background noise and, by the conclusion, becomes an ominous sign of horror lurking just upstairs. Olivia Hussey, Andrea Martin, and Margot Kidder star as sorority sisters plagued by increasingly threatening phone calls and eventually a mysterious and brutal killer. Though the film is utterly simple and lacks any thorough explanations of the killer's identity or motive, it is surprisingly effective in how it takes just a handful of elements and combines them to create an undeniably frightening film. In the end, it doesn't need to unmask the person committing the murders, and for very good reason; what he does is far more horrifying than who he is.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Black Christmas</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I really only heard there was an original film hearing about the remake, but this was is given four stars out of five, so I thought I might as well give it a try, from director Bob Clark (Porky's). Basically at a Canadian college campus the sorority house are planning their Christmas holiday, and they start things off with a big party. Throughout the night the house phone keeps ringing, it is a strange anonymous voice putting them on the edge with his weird high-pitched animal type noises and foul language. Soon enough things get worse, when Clare Harrison (Lynne Griffin) disappears, and the remaining girls call the police, who don't express much interest in helping. Meanwhile, Jess Bradford (Olivia Hussey) wants an abortion, going against the wishes of boyfriend Peter Smythe (Keir Dullea), and the police eventually decide to help finding the body of a 13-year-old girl. After all the other girls have disappeared, including Barbara 'Barb' Coard (Margot Kidder), Phyllis 'Phyl' Carlson (Andrea Martin) and housekeeper Mrs. 'Mac' MacHenry (Marian Waldman), Jess is the only one left. It is a psychopath committing all these scary acts, but we know it is worse when Lt. Kenneth Fuller (John Saxon) is told that the obscene phone calls are coming from inside the house. Also starring Art Hindle as Chris Hayden and James Edmond as Mr. Harrison. It had a catchy tag-line, "If this picture doesn't make your skin crawl, it's on too tight!", but the film itself isn't very catchy. The performances are okay, the death scenes are average, and the scares are pretty mild, I can't see fully how this deserves four stars, but I suppose it is something different than a cheery Christmas film, not a bad seasonal horror thriller. Good!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2009-03-17</date>
		<username>Superunknovvn</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Two outlooks, two different ratings</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Black Christmas" was a pioneering movie. It's safe to say that this was one of the first if not THE first slasher-flick of all time. As such, the movie has a lot going for itself. Everything that would later become a cliché is established here: the POV-shot of the killer entering rooms, the creative ways of picking off victims one by one, the final girl, etc. "Black Christmas" invented it all. On top of that the movie has top notch direction and an artful cinematography, which is sometimes reminiscent of Dario Argento's work. The acting is fine for the most part, and the mysterious killer and his creepy phone calls give Michael Myers a run for the money. So, if we take all that into consideration, "Black Christmas" is a genuine horror masterpiece.However, a lot of time has passed and a lot of slasher movies have been made since 1974. If you watch this movie now for the first time, it won't do much for you. You've seen these ideas before and probably in a more shocking way. "Black Christmas" may have been the first movie to do it, but other movies were the ones who made the basic slasher-elements famous.That's hardly director Bob Clark's fault, of course. It's just that if you're not a cineaste, you won't care too much about whether or not this movie was inventive when it first came out. All you'll care about is whether it can surprise and entertain you still, and truth be told, if you've seen the "Halloween"- and "Friday The 13th"-flicks or any movies of their ilk, then "Black Christmas" probably won't blow you away.However, if you do care about historical movie facts, then you must see Bob Clark's true holiday classic (the "false" one being "A Christmas Story" from 1983). After all, "Psycho" is also respected for the effect it originally had on audiences mostly. Even Hitchcock's masterpiece seems tame and predictable from today's point of view, so why should you be more critical when it comes to judging "Black Christmas"? For horror buffs, this one is a must, anyway.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>JoeKarlosi</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Black Christmas (1974) ***</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The attic of a girl's sorority house during the holiday season becomes a nesting place for a grunting maniac, who scales the trellis one evening and begins to murder women amidst the snow and good cheer of Christmas carols. The madman uses the telephone to call the girls and taunts them with noises and deranged ramblings in a very unsettling manner. Olivia Hussey stars as the main innocent girl who is on the receiving end, with Margot Kidder as one of her no-nonsense loudmouth friends. The dependable John Saxon takes on the part of a police lieutenant who tries to help.The first thing I want to say about the film is that it's not unlike everything we've seen since in later slasher movies. Every cliché is present: the heavy-breathing psycho, the obscene phone calls; the terrified girl on the other end who is incredibly stupid; the grisly murder techniques; the killer's point of view shots... There is a sense of the familiar here, and I was almost prepared to give the movie a half-star less rating until I reminded myself .. this film came first, BEFORE all those other clones. Director Bob Clark has said that John Carpenter liked this film, and we can see some of its influence in Carpenter's HALLOWEEN, which often gets all the credit. But it's not the fault of the more rare BLACK Christmas that it spawned all the copycats which make it seem like one of them itself; I'd imagine this trend setter felt much more cutting edge in 1974, and that's why I gave it the extra boost it deserves. There is good and bad here ... I have yet to enjoy Margot Kidder in anything, and her drunken sleaze-mouth character here is annoying, as is the fat female den mom who's got a bottle of booze hidden everywhere in the house ... but not to worry. The killer, who's identity is a mystery, manages to be scary just through his eerie phone calls and creepy sounds. The idea of all this gruesome mayhem as a parallel to the joyous Christmas events happening on the side achieves the proper disturbing effect. The look of the large spooky house from the outside, complete with the image in the attic window, is haunting. The movie is not completely original, but it deserves extra credit for setting up so many now-patented and tried and true horror film clichés. *** out of ****</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2009-02-01</date>
		<username>LuckyMatt93</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A genuine horror masterpiece</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is not just the godfather of all slashers, it's the godfather of all horror films. Black Christmas is a sharp edged gem that sadly never garnered the popularity that it's inferior ripoffs like Halloween did and in consequence never got the praise it much deserved. Depressing, really depressing.The director, Bob Clark, takes his time setting up a dark mood, and exploiting his eerie setting fully while giving us hints of slick style on the way (the killer POV shots and the slow motion). He also inter-cuts murder sequences with mundane events which makes the scenes quite effective. He gives us a solid script, excellent old fashioned suspense, and a strong atmosphere to rely on that oozes with an unrelenting creepiness.Character development sets this one apart from other horror films. Olivia Hussey is angelic as the film's resident Scream Queen. Her character, Jessica, is thinking about having an abortion; she's definitely not your modern day leading lady for a slasher film, which usually involves someone pure at heart, innocent and virginal. These troubles that Jessica goes through add depth to her character, making her impossible not to like and adding the reality of character flaws to the whole picture. The rest of the characters are given a lot of depth as well, and this really helps you sympathize for them.Music, like Halloween, is just as important of a factor as any horror classic. Perhaps it has to do with Peter, Jessica's boyfriend, being a pianist, that the scoring is a low and aggressive piano set. It's the part of the film that makes your creep bone crawl. Jessica receives a phone call from the killer, who shouts out "Billy!" "Agnes!" "What your mother and I must know is..." and other disturbing, seemingly meaningless quotes. His voice is dark, almost demonic, and uses multiple high and low tones that sometimes overlap each other. The question often comes up of whether or not the killer called "Billy", is possessed, or just very talented at what he does. The 2006 remake of Black Christmas, Black X-Mas, tries to answer every single question of what "Billy" is saying in the original, and explain his background. To do so completely destroys the purpose of Bob Clark's film, as the scariest aspect of Black Christmas is all of the things that you don't know. We enjoy wondering who the killer is, what his motive exactly is, where he came from, do we know him already? What is his motive? Where did he come from? Do we know him already? The film gives us just enough information and clues to chew on without providing straight out concrete answers. This allows the film to linger in our minds as we try to figure out what our own opinions are as to it's many mysteries. It's all in the mind, which is where the BEST creativity comes from (Blair Witch Project, Rosemary's Baby, etc). The lesser we see and the lesser we know, the better.Black Christmas was made before the thousands of clichés of the slasher genre became typical, and has impacted American slashers like few others. Halloween has the mystery of who the killer is nearly down pat; it was a holiday horror with a stalker and anonymous phone callers. When a Stranger Calls took the nerve smashing concept from Black Christmas and used it for the opening act, where "the calls were coming from inside the house." Many slashers to follow would use Black Christmas' "Is that you?" line when a character would wonder who is lurking about. Today, the creepy caller often gets spoofed (Student Bodies) or updated in the form of a less effective cell phone (Scream). This Canadian terror has influenced SO SO many artistic gems that it feels like a crime that it is rarely seen as the powerhouse it deserves to be acknowledged as. No doubt, if Bob Clark could've copyrighted the slasher movie concepts and clichés that he created, he would've been just as famous as John Carpenter or Wes Craven, maybe even more.It's simple (like Halloween) and doesn't require any additions to make it stronger. It's damn near flawless for gods sake! It's a truly fantastic masterpiece of horror cinema, the best horror film ever made. We never find out the answers to the killings at the house. This may be frustrating to some, but it's really a concept of genius, keeping the evil untouched and ready to haunt our minds on the night of December 24th.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Classic horror, see it now.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Here's the one I watched on Halloween but couldn't understand due to all the ruckus going on at the party. And now that I have sat down and watched it properly, I see exactly what makes this one such a classic.Black Christmas is one of those old school horror movies that knows what it's doing, unlike mostly every horror movie that comes out these days. The build-up is long and it sets a tangible mood - this is not a movie trying to rush itself into the blood and gore, which is not really one of the focal points of this movie anyway. Yes, there are stock characters, and there is some thin human conflict going on between them, but you actually care about these characters, and you don't really want to see them die. What a novel concept, right? This makes it all the more disturbing as the stalker's phone calls escalate in insanity and as the bodies begin to pile up. The acting is really good for these kinds of movies, too, with great performances from all of the characters.And goddammit, those phone calls get really, really creepy! I mean, I know what you're thinking; "oh, wow, a phone call, how can that be scary," but no, it is scary. I won't spoil the reasons why for those who haven't seen this gem yet, but the atmosphere of the whole thing is blackened, haunting, claustrophobic and memorable as Hell. This is all very believable, leaving no room for anyone to laugh and pan this movie off as unrealistic or silly. This could happen to anyone! And that, in a similar manner to Halloween, is perhaps the scariest thing about this movie.From before a time when horror equated to ridiculous special effects, gory amputated limbs and torture chambers, Black Christmas hits you hard no matter where or when you watch it, with a ferociously creepy mood, a calculating, dark atmosphere and just enough pizazz to have you looking under your bed this Christmas eve to make sure that there aren't any bogeymen coming to get you. Highly recommended.</fullReview>

Olivia Hussey and Margot Kidder (also look for Andrea Martin of SCTV fame) star as two residents of a sorority house that is emptying out as Christmas approaches. The atmosphere is jolly and carefree, except for an ongoing series of menacing telephone calls, and, oh yes, we've just seen someone climb into the attic with apparent ill intent. Kidder does some scene-stealing as the bad girl, Hussey illustrates one of the downsides to having beautiful long '70s hair, and Keir Dullea does a nice turn as the creepy boyfriend. Director Robert Clark knows that the unseen is far scarier than what can be seen and he ratchets up the tension beautifully, making good use of ominous shadows, and putting in nice touches like replacing the sound of a distraught woman's scream with the piercing ring of yet another ominous phone call. This is a terrific, well-made little movie that is genuinely sleep-with-the-lights-on scary. Don't miss it.</fullReview>

		<date>2008-01-01</date>
		<username>hypostylin</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Sensational Slasher Fare</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Don't ever confuse this with the antagonistically wretched 2006 remake. The original Black Christmas is a masterful, progenitive slasher film that constantly tests my loyalty to John Carpenter's Halloween. Like that latter film, Black Christmas' strength is in its simplicity: a demented killer is making disturbing (and I mean DISTURBING) phone calls to a snowbound Canadian sorority house, sleepily populated by a skeleton crew of Christmas break stragglers. Little do they know, the calls are coming from inside the house.A major trick that elevates the simple plot, however, is that, even though we know from the get go that the calls are coming from inside the sorority house, the film is scant on so many other details that we feel as in the dark and uneasy as the characters. Our interest in the film is sustained throughout because we want to know what happens to these people.That brings me to my next point. Surely odd for a slasher film, the characters in Black Christmas are well-drawn and the performances that fill them are top-notch. Olivia Hussey (one of the most beautiful women to ever grace the screen) and Margot Kidder are, of course, the obvious stand-outs, as the grave and level-headed Jess and drunken, sailor-mouthed Barb respectively. However, there are other remarkable players here, including Marian Waldman in a delightfully hilarious role, genre veteran John Saxon, and Art Hindle, who went on to play a fantastic leading man in David Cronenberg's not-so-fantastic The Brood.Overall, the film is seeping with classic '70s style and atmosphere. Even the minimalist score, with its quiet, brooding sustained notes, reveals a bygone flair for film-making. Lastly, the phone calls the killer makes have to be heard to be believed. If you were scared by Regan's possessed ramblings in The Exorcist, Billy's psychotic shrieks will have you trembling for days.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2007-11-01</date>
		<username>Jonny_Numb</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>A Black Christmas Story</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Bob Clark, whose life was cut short earlier this year by a freak accident, will always be a genre hero; after all, this is the man who gave us the controversial Vietnam-horror allegory "Deathdream" (1974), the playful Romero homage "Children Shouldn't Play With Dead Things" (1972), and the celebrated holiday comedy, "A Christmas Story" (1983). "Black Christmas," a seasonal slasher that predated "Halloween" by 4 years, establishes the formula with grace, subtlety, and skill, threaded with Clark's strong sense of small-town Americana (shots are composed with the biographical artistry of a Norman Rockwell painting). An obscene phone-caller is harassing the girls of a sorority house; one of the girls and the housemother (an inspired comic performance by Marian Waldman) go missing; and our protagonist, Jess (Olivia Hussey) is looking to get an abortion, much to the objection of her boyfriend Peter (Keir Dullea). Happy holidays, indeed! Roy Moore's screenplay takes a simple premise and lets the layers play out naturally--the collegiate and small-town humor still resonates today, and the characters are played with a minimum of exaggeration (as I said about the Clark-produced "Deranged," we feel like we KNOW these people); my only complaint is that not all of the characters are well-integrated, and some disappear for long stretches at a time. Otherwise, we have some iconic figures that clearly influenced Carpenter's babysitters: Hussey as the Final Girl, Margot Kidder as the hard-drinking joker, and Andrea Martin as the straight- yet-slightly-gawky good girl. The legendary John Saxon is his usual excellent self as the local police captain. And Clark/Cronenberg regular Les Carlson ("Deranged," "A Christmas Story," "Videodrome") has a small role that is the crux of most of the film's suspense (he's the phone-company employee tracking the source of the obscene calls through switchboards as high as the stacks at the Library of Congress). "Black Christmas" is one of the best slashers out there, and certainly one of the most influential (next to "Psycho")--raise some eyebrows at this year's holiday gathering by putting this on right after "A Christmas Story" (and vive la difference!).</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>I'll never look at Christmas the same way again</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>For years I knew little about this forgotten classic. I'd seen the box for it in video stores, with that creepy image of a girl in a chair, head covered tightly by a plastic bag, and a large wreath around the whole image. But for some reason I never rented it and didn't get the urge to until the remake was about to come out and I knew I had to see the original first before even thinking about seeing the new one, so I just slapped down a few bucks and bought it. Sure I could have ended up being one of few who didn't like the film and would have totally wasted my money, but this was not the case. I watched it alone, with the lights off, and fell in love. Now to be honest I still think John Carpenter's 'Halloween,' which came four years later, is even better, but Black Christmas came first and deserves just as much attention if not more.From frame one I was engrossed in it. Unlike a lot of horror films we see today, this one feels very real and like it could actually take place. That is what great horror is all about. The setting, the characters, the villain, and so on, all very real. Having regular, everyday things going on in the background also helps in making this more than just another stalk-and-slash flick. Having been used to gory, no-brainer slasher movies, this was like a breath of fresh air.Pros: Atmospheric direction by director Clark. Plenty of creepiness, especially those phone calls. Well-acted by a then largely unknown cast of actors and actresses. A good sense of humor. A very threatening villain that, thankfully, is kept in the shadows. There's a feeling of dread hanging over the whole film. A subtle, but effective music score and great sound effects work. Moves at a slow, but steady pace. Suspenseful and full of good twists and turns. Beautiful to look at in terms of cinematography and production design. Cons: A bit dated now. Final thoughts: As a horror buff I hate admitting this, but I didn't see this film for years and years. When I heard a remake was on the way I felt I really needed to see the original first, and am glad I finally did. Now I see what everyone was talking about when they said this film paved the way for future slasher films. Sure I love 'Halloween' and it too deserves recognition, but Black Christmas came first. Also, as many others have said, I hope that the remake encourages those who haven't seen the original to check it out.My rating: 5/5</fullReview>

		<username>gavin6942</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>The Horror Film That Started It All</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Before Bob Clark made "Porky's" or "A Christmas Story", he made this lost classic: "Black Christmas". A sorority house is infiltrated by a serial killer who is probably mentally deranged. Being Christmas, the police are overburdened and the girls are drunk. Who can save them? Can they be saved? This film excels at everything. The mood is very good. The casting is great (Olivia Hussey is fantastic, and a young Margot Kidder steals the show). The movie keeps you on the edge of your seat all the way to the end. This really lays the groundwork for films to come.And this is what makes "Black Christmas" so great. Besides a foul-mouthed Santa Claus and the feeding of alcohol to children, we have the beginning of some of the most well-known horror clichés. "Halloween" borrows the first person camera angles, and all slashers in general take something from this movie (since, to my knowledge, this was the first movie featuring drunk kids hacked up by an outside intruder)."When A Stranger Calls" borrows heavily, as does "Scream". The horror comedy "Student Bodies" (now very hard to find, and not even on Netflix at all) takes so much from "Black Christmas" it should have made them royalties. In short, once you've seen this horror film you've really seen them all.Some have said the music was noteworthy. Personally, I didn't notice the music -- but I'll just assume that means it fit the mood well. What I did notice was the voice of The Moaner. Films with phantom callers are not unusual, but I don't think any of them do what this film does with the creep. Some strange calls.There is a subplot of a killed girl and of a possible abortion. These story lines never get resolved but work to reinforce the tension in the air. And unlike every other horror film, there is no "Black Christmas 2". I commend them for that choice... will the remakers make the same decision? If you haven't seen this film, see it. You've been eating your hot fudge sundaes without the ice cream, and it's time for that to change.</fullReview>
	</review>

They're my friend's Black &amp; Tan Coonhounds.Just a quirky detail.</fullReview>

		<date>2006-10-05</date>
		<username>Phantasm01</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A great slasher film - Sadly underrated</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Horror films have a bad reputation for being completely senseless and lacking a sense of artistic integrity. I'm not going to lie, some of them do fall into that category. However, there are moments in cinema history when you get a film that defines the genre. Something that is so good that it transcends the usual trappings that hold films of its kind down. A Nightmare on Elm Street, Halloween, The Exorcist, Alien, and The Texas Chainsaw Massacre are just a few of the horror gems that are well made and also very scary.The story is quite simple. At a sorority house, the girls are getting ready to go home for Christmas but begin receiving phone calls from a strange caller who won't give his name. The next day, many girls leave, but one who should have met her father doesn't turn up, which causes great concern for her safety. When another girl goes missing, the police begin searching the area and find a body nearby. Meanwhile, with only three students left in the sorority house, the phone calls continue, getting more and more menacing each time, but unknown to the remaining members of the house, the caller, and perhaps the killer, is closer to them than their nightmares could ever imagine.Keep in the mind that the killer calling his victims before he strikes them practically began here. Some might tell you it started with the original When A Stranger Calls, which was actually released a few years later, and it certainly took place before Scream, which was released in 1996. Black Christmas was the first to effectively use this technique and I must say this film uses it the best. While, menacing phone calls can get tiresome in some thrillers, the calls in this film generate fear and tension with each one that is made. They gradually become more violent and disturbing, to the point that they're almost unbearable. This isn't someone merely playing a game, this is someone with deep emotional and mental problems. The phone actually becomes a character itself because for most of the film it's our only link to the killer. We learn very little about him but what we do learn about him is through his acting out on the phone. I guess you can say the phone is an extension of his character.I also liked that this film believe less is more. There is very little blood and most of the violence is implied. Sometimes a visceral approach to horror can work but I think horror is most effective when they leave a lot of it up to the imagination. Your mind certainly creates a more disturbing image then they might've even shown.Speaking of the killer, he's probably one of the better ones to appear in a film of this nature. The most fascinating thing about him is you don't know anything about him throughout the film. The fact that he has randomly chosen to strike these girls makes him all the more frightening because it proves that sometimes crime is unexplained and completely unprovoked. You never really get a clear look at him, since he's merely a voice on the phone or cleverly shrouded in darkness but he's still a very powerful presence. The fact that he's seen rarely makes his few big "appearances" all the more terrifying. I'll provide two pictures of his most frightening exposures in the film and i guarantee when you see it, even after seeing these, those images will still stay with you long after the film is over.Halloween is commonly known for its effective opening scene which a steadycam shot that is supposed to be the perspective of the killer coming upon the house. That technique was made popular in that film but it was used first here and it's actually used often to great effect. The camera is constantly giving you the sense that danger is approaching and it's a very simple but effective technique. A lot of the film is seen from his perspective so it becomes very interesting to view a film through the killer's eyes.No good horror film can work without engaging leads and Black Christmas certainly has a pretty good cast. Olivia Hussy, who at this time was very popular due to her turn as Juliet in Romeo and Juliet, is a very likable heroine. She differs from most because she's definitely flawed and is nowhere near perfect but it also makes her all the more human. She's able to carry the film and while she's not the strongest actress around, she certainly handles the task at hand very well. The rest of the sorority girl cast consist of mostly unknowns but Margot Kidder, who would go on to play Lois Lane, is featured as one and quite funny as the booze hound of the house. John Saxon, of A Nightmare on Elm Street fame, appears here as a detective and is always a welcomed presence in anything he's in.Black Christmas is pretty hard to find in stores. I had to get mine on amazon but if you can try to catch this film. It's simple, effective and very scary. It should be given more credit for its role in shaping the slasher genre and maybe with the release of the remake this year, the original will get its much deserved mainstream attention. Check out Black Christmas this Halloween season, you won't be disappointed.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Jerichoholics_Anonymous</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Great slasher film, one of the best, but it's no Halloween...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A lot of people talk about this film like it is the true unsung hero of the stalker/slasher film genre, even over the well-known and championed Halloween. I do think this is a great film and it does put most slasher films to shame, but it never comes close to honestly beating Halloween in terms of overall product.Some great directing and camera-work, not bad acting, bizarre and manic killer with no motive other than insanity and a few nice twists to keep the viewer interested make this a must-own film if you are a fan of the genre. However, I must insist, this is not quite as good as Halloween. Michael Myers' relentless and emotionless stalking beats the cackling, manic, obscene phone calling killer hands down. Add a superior soundtrack and you have a superior product, in my opinion.I noticed a few people on the IMDb making such audacious comments as: 'Halloween copied/ripped-off Black Christmas'. If I may be frank, that couldn't be more wrong. The only real similarity comes with the camera-work at the beginning of the film. This camera technique employed the pan-a-glide camera and Halloween made much better use of it and the POV shot. I can't put my finger on it and don't know exactly why but there was something distinctly wrong with the shots where we are supposed to be looking through the killer's eyes. In Halloween it felt right, in this film it felt wrong. Perhaps something to do with over-use of the technique. Halloween didn't use it quite as often and didn't have to rely on it quite so much, as it has suspense coming out of its ears from the amazing soundtrack and terrifying on-screen killer. Also, Halloween used more static camera shots watching potential victims, implying Michael Myers was watching but not confirming it, whereas in Black Christmas you KNEW he the killer was watching, because you could hear his breathing and the camera moved around much more. I know this is not the same in the opening sequence of Halloween, but throughout the rest of the film I feel the techniques are superior than Black Christmas.Still, this film IS great and if you do like Halloween, or any films of this genre, I urge you to buy this as it is brilliant.Ultimately, I think the difference is that Black Christmas is the start of the 'slasher' film genre, whereas Halloween was more a 'stalker' film. In Black Christmas, the killer never STALKS his victims. Even the female protagonist when she runs from the killer, isn't really stalked by him as such, as she hardly even runs away until the end of the film. The killer in this film waits for his victims to come to him and the suspense works in this way, where you wait for them to walk into their demise. In Halloween, the killer is actively stalking his victims, following Laurie during the day, going to Laurie's friends houses at night and killing them. So in this respect I guess that if you are more of a fan of the 'killer hiding in the closet' type of movie, this is for you, but if you are more a fan of the stalker type of movie, you will prefer Halloween. Or ask yourself this: what do you find more scary: a killer who you never actually see, even in the final stages of the film, or a silent but deadly shape, who remains off-camera until he strikes, relentlessly stalking his victim for no other reason than that he is pure Evil?But again, I really urge you to watch BOTH! Last note: Female protagonist in this film is FIT. MUCH fitter than Laurie Strode, so that's reason enough why you should watch this!</fullReview>
	</review>

	<review>
		<date>2006-05-01</date>
		<username>Woodyanders (Woodyanders@aol.com)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A splendidly scary &amp; spooky 70's seasonal slasher landmark</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Made a good four years prior to John Carpenter's phenomenally hair-raising terror movie watershed "Halloween," this superbly scary and suspenseful ahead-of-its-time seasonal slasher trailblazer laid the basic groundwork for the countless similarly plotted holiday-themed horror entries which followed in its influential wake throughout the late 70's and up until the mid 80's.The then fresh and novel, now stale and generic premise centers on a frightfully maniacal cackling lunatic who escapes from an asylum and holes up in the dusty attic of a nearby college sorority house on December 24th. At first the babbling, profanity-screaming nutjob taunts the sorority girls with disturbing obscene phone calls before proceeding to pick off the hapless lasses one at a time. Eventually a token lovely coed (sympathetically played by the astonishingly gorgeous Olivia Hussey) has to face the murderous kook on her own (said only lady left heroine is called in slasher movie parlance "the final girl," which later became a huge cliché in subsequent slice'n'dice features). Director Benjamin "Bob" Clark, who had previously helmed the superior zombie pictures "Deathdream" and "Children Shouldn't Play With Dead Things" (Clark's latter efforts include the first two uproariously raunchy "Porky's" films and, amazingly enough, the disarmingly sweet yuletide treat "A Christmas Story"!) masterfully creates and sustains a genuinely eerie and nerve-jangling atmosphere while establishing such standard body count movie components as hand-held subjective camera shots of the killer on the prowl, bravura murder set pieces (suffocation with plastic bag, impalement on hook, and, in the single most stunning kill scene, a comely honey being stabbed to death with a crystal unicorn ornament that's brilliantly inter-cut with an angelic children's choir singing "Sleep in Heavenly Peace" with positively haunting and harrowing results), a creepy mystery wacko whose true identity is never revealed, a few red herring secondary characters (notably Keir Dullea as Hussey's neurotic, violently temperamental pianist boyfriend), a riveting last reel showdown with a brave and resourceful heroine asserting her proto-feminist strength and independence by doing in the killer all by her lonesome, and a socko surprise ending.Cleverly written by Ray Moore, the neatly twisty script further adds several riotous dollops of bawdy humor to leaven the unnerving tension and an intelligent reason why the initial murder victims aren't immediately missed (they left early for the holidays). Reginald Morris' strikingly crisp and agile cinematography vividly captures the stark bleakness of the severely cold wintry landscape, thereby greatly enhancing the overall literally and figuratively chilling mood. The cast all turn in bang-up performances: Margot Kidder vamps it up hilariously as a hysterically brash and slutty tramp, Marion Waldman likewise amuses as the outrageously vulgar den mother, John Saxon is properly stalwart in the first of many concerned police chief parts (a role he reprised to perfection in "A Nightmare on Elm Street"), "The Brood" 's Art Hindle lurks around the edges as a hunky frat boy, David Cronenberg regular Art ("Videodrome," "The Fly") Hindle pops up as a helpful cop, Doug McGrath supplies side-splitting comic relief as a geeky desk sergeant, and a pre-"SCTV" Andrea Martin is engaging as a mousy sorority sister. Smart, absorbing and above all profoundly frightening, "Black Christmas" will most assuredly give you the willies any old time of the year.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>LoneWolfAndCub</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>One of the BEST horror films</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Black Christmas is a very underrated and un-recognised Canadian horror film from director Bob Clarke (probably most known for the raunchy comedy Porkys). This came before Halloween, Friday the 13th, When A Stranger Calls or A Nightmare on Elm Street. This is what started the slasher genre. This created the killer's POV shots, the creepy scores and the obscene phone calls.There really isn't a huge plot here, it is basically about a group of girls in a sorority house are being terrorised by obscene phone calls. No one has any idea who it is calling them. Slowly, each one goes missing and everyone becomes more aware of what is going on.Despite the minimal plot, this is THE slasher film. It has an extremely creepy score that will have the hairs on your neck standing, very effective cinematography (the house is perfect), a great group of younger, great camera work and older actresses/actors and it packs a walloping twist at the end. The lack of gore or violence suits the movie PERFECTLY. If they had shown the killings in full detail, it would not have been as good. One murder (involving a glass unicorn) is the stand-out, being one of the most recognised murder scenes in horror movie history.Many people consider Halloween the original slasher, that is not the case. BLACK Christmas is the ORIGINAL slasher. But it is much more than that. Instead of a cast of complete asses, we have a group of smarter people. They die not because of idiocy, but because of accident or just the wrong place in the wrong time. There is also some great humour tied in. Margot Kidder being the funniest of them all.The movie gives me the chills, especially the ending, which is the greatest in my opinion, of any horror movie. I urge anyone who considers themselves a horror fan to see this.5/5.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Genuinely scary and different</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'll start by saying what everyone else has already said about Black Christmas. Imagine its 1974, you haven't seen Halloween, Friday the 13th or Scream. The term "slasher movie" barely exists. Now watch Black Christmas and you'll see just how innovative and scary it was and still is.An unknown figure breaks into the attic of a sorority house as it clears out for the holidays. Amongst those left are; Jess, who is pregnant and wants an abortion and Barb who is a foul-mouthed good-time-girl. Before long, the house mates receive some VERY obscene phone calls (which are virtually the sole reason for its rating). Then, one of the girls disappears...Whilst this may seem generic, it is still pretty groundbreaking by today's standards. Most of the killings are never shown and corpses are never found; the park-search scene is very effective for instance. Characterisation is strong; Jess' abortion and her boyfriend's despair at it is pretty dramatic. The level of humour is unusual, without edging into comedy. The killer himself (who we never see at all) is one of the most frightening characters in cinema; a nut-case who screams wildly, reenacts bizarre domestic situations and has a very disturbing back-story to do with a baby.The script is fresh, the direction is tight and is basically just damn scary. Black Chistmas is arguably my favourite horror film because of these reasons.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Wonderful horror movie with an absolutely horrific bit of trivia</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is by far one of the best and most unknown horror movies I have ever seen. The atmosphere, the music, the plot, the ending... all of it is absolutely perfect.But what really creeps me out about Black Christmas is the fact that it seems like a blueprint for serial killer Ted Bundy's attack at the Chi Omega sorority house in 1978. Bundy was known for loving slasher movies and he fantasized almost 24/7 about violence against women. In fact, he was once shown the movie Friday the 13th in prison and he couldn't finish it because he got too overstimulated. So it's no stretch of the imagination to think Bundy saw this movie in 1974 and enjoyed every single minute of it.Especially if he was taking notes.So if this movie wasn't creepy enough on its own, just realize it probably served as an inspiration for one of the most notorious serial killings in American history. A lot of the details from Chi Omega are pretty darn close, particularly what happens to Margot Kidder. And if that isn't enough to make your skin crawl, you must be a thicker skinned person than me!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A dark holiday, a darker killer</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Christmas is known for the holiday of giving, but in this case it is the holiday for murder, as a loose killer enters a sorority house, hides in the attic and commences disturbing phone calls to the girls downstairs. The voice of the killer is elusive while at the same time quite chilling and unorthodox. Eventually, the killer begins picking off its victims one by one, and everyone is clueless as to who is responsible for these mysterious murders, suspenseful enough?Black Christmas clearly inspired countless clones, and like those worthless imitations, Black Christmas has its flaws and unexplained conclusions. The viewer never discovers the true identity of the killer, and though the motives are not revealed, eerie angle shots and offbeat music is replaced instead. Original? For that time, definitely.Spoilers...Spoilers...SpoilersFollowing one of the murders, the killer phones below and speaks with one of the girls depicting the horror and dismay inflicted upon its victims before their untimely deaths. Then, ambiguity is created as one of the girl's boyfriend closely resembles the killer by shadow, but this is only an illusion employed to hoodwink the viewer. Clever? You bet. Now, during the climax of the film, the girl goes upstairs against the orders of the police to get her sorority sisters until confronted by the killer. This scene is quite spooky, but nonsensical, because the girl was told that the phone calls were within the house, so going any other place in there is a stupid move. A folly on behalf of the director or a purposeful maneuver to show the lack of common sense on the part of the girl. Either way, the scene is set up for the viewer to take a look at the "eye" of the killer, and the vague revealing of its identity as "Billy" the other personality of one of the characters in the movie. The viewer is left trying to decide if the killer is the girl's strange boyfriend because of his lurking about the house, and his shadowing in the background, but it's not him! This is found out at the very end!The multiple voicings of the killer shows the schizophrenic mind of the killer at work, and motiveless reasons behinds its slayings. The killer takes great pleasure in tormenting its victims not only in its head, but also on paper. However, the killer lives on as we all find out with the girl escaping the attack, but lying drug induced in the house. Not very smart there, but the cops must believe the killer's dead, since the boyfriend was slain by the girl in self-defense. The bodies of the victims remain upstairs as entertaining tools for the killer, and adds more schizoids to its already depraved, twisted mind. An open-ending no doubt. Overall, the movie has its moments of dread and suspense via atmosphere and creepy score, but there are clearly loose pieces that are never clarified, unfortunately. Rating **</fullReview>

		<username>Bjorn (ODDBear)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A must see horror film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Predating Halloween and it's countless clones in the stalk'n slash genre, I have to say this movie really serves up the goods. Sure, to today's audience it may seem a bit slow and dated but to the true fan, this is a masterpiece of the genre. A madman hides in the attic of a sorority house, telephones the girls from upstairs and eventually starts killing some of them.So many elements have been lifted from this film and utilized to great effect, think of When A Stranger Calls, Halloween, Scream and countless sorority slashers to name a few, this movie has to be termed as somewhat of a groundbreaker. Filmed with a low budget, director Clark relies solely on mood, lighting and atmosphere to give the film it's edge. He succeeds brilliantly. Apart from a rather tedious 10-15 minute segment involving the housekeeper of the sorority the film absolutely keeps it's tension filled grip on the viewer and doesn't let go at all. I even found myself shouting at the tube, telling the girl "don't do this, don't go there", and I can't remember ever doing this before or after. Watching it alone (plus, it was at Christmas time) the film totally freaked me out. Acting is way above average for a slasher flick. Olivia Hussey, besides being a total knockout, is very good in the lead role. Margot "Lois Lane" Kidder is a delight as the foul mouthed drunk, Keir Dullea fairly creepy as Hussey's not-so-sane boyfriend and genre favourite John Saxon is good as always. For the most part, the humour goes down well also. One particularly funny scene involving a not so bright cop, but that seems to be a regular in Clark's films. If you're a fan of horror films, it doesn't get much better than this. Highly recommended.</fullReview>

		<username>KuRt-33 (kurtaerden@yahoo.com)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Sorority girls shouldn't play with mad killers</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Best known for directing "Porky's", Bob Clark also directed "Porky's II", "Baby Geniuses" and "Baby Geniuses 2". To be fair, he's also the director of a decent zombie film with an excellent title: "Children Shouldn't Play With Dead Things". And of course of "Black Christmas", a film that has all good films should have: an inspired screenplay, good actors and excellent camera work.Olivia Hussey is the star of the film. She was mainly cast and admired because of her leading role in Zeffirelli's version of "Romeo And Juliet". "Black Christmas" isn't the weirdest film Hussey is in: you can also spot her in Metzger's "The Cat And The Canary", Kinji Fukasaku's "Fukkatsu no hi" (a.k.a. "Virus") and "Turkey Shoot" (a.k.a. "Blood Camp Thatcher"). You'd almost be more surprised she recently played Mother Teresa. Olivia Hussey is excellent in "Black Christmas" as Jessica (Jess) Bradford, the pregnant and terrorized girl in the sorority house. Don't be put off by the fact that "Black Christmas" takes place in a sorority house: this isn't the sort of film where scantily-clad bimbos are chased by a maniac with a set of knives. The film actually bothered about something called 'plot' and most characters have (at least a little) depth. A welcome change.Also noteworthy is the camera work. The hand-held camera allows us to look through the eyes of the killer, four years before John Carpenter's "Halloween" did the same thing. The cameraman had to climb up and down the occasional stairs, which often looks silly because of a hand-held camera, but "Black Christmas" does an okay job here.Back to the cast: Keir Dullea ("2001: A Space Odyssey" and a handful of cult movies), Margot Kidder ("Superman" and "Sisters") and Art Hindle (best remembered for being in Cronenberg's "The Brood", but also in Clark's "Porky's" films) are just a few actors in a film where most people do at least a decent job. A special mention should be reserved for John Saxon (who starred in more cult films than one could name), who accepted the role of Lt. Fuller when the original actor (Edmond O'Brien) seemed unfit for the role. Saxon flew to Ontario and rushed to the set, just in time for his first scene (in the middle of the night).One might argue why "Black Christmas" needed the subplot of the missing girl, but it's effectively used in a handful of scenes. The naive policeman is there for comic relief, but not the brainless sort of humour one often finds in horror films. If you look at the IMDb message board for "Black Christmas" (but don't do that before you've seen the film) you'll notice the film is vague enough about the identity of the killer, which allows the viewer to guess whether the killer is one of the main characters. So is (s)he? For the answer to that question, I refer you to "Black Christmas", one of the better horror films of the 1970s. Don't expect too much, just sit back and let the film work itself towards an exciting climax and finale.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2003-11-29</date>
		<username>Gafke</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The Original Lost Slasher Film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>3 years before "Halloween" forever changed the horror film genre, "Black Christmas" was the original slasher film...and what a weird-ass film it is! There is a killer hiding in the attic of a posh sorority house, a killer whose face we never see and whose motives are never revealed. The film begins almost immediately with the sickest, most obscene phone call ever put on film. It's not so much what the caller has to say (although, for 1974, it's pretty graphic, sickening stuff!) but how he says it; his (or her?) voice changes with every sentence, sometimes two or three voices overlapping each other. It is indeed a chilling thing to hear, reminding me very strongly of Reagan MacNeils Legion of voices in "The Exorcist." The call ends with the mans voice, now speaking in a normal (but no less frightening) baritone, informing Margot Kidder that he is going to kill her. The spree begins almost as soon as the call ends.All of the characters are rather offbeat, and all of them suspect. Olivia Hussey is the surprisingly non-virginal and newly pregnant heroine. Keir Dullea is her moody and slightly obsessive piano playing boyfriend who refuses to allow her to get an abortion. Margot Kidder is the drunk, rude sorority sister with a mouth only slightly less foul than that of the obscene caller, who continues to phone and torment our confused and increasingly frightened Miss Hussey. The girls continue to disappear, but no bodies are ever discovered and no one has any reason to climb up to the attic, where a display of horror awaits. There are some ingenious killings here: a plastic bag, a hook on a rope, the glass horn of a unicorn, all very wince-inducing. The climax obviously went on to inspire "When A Stranger Calls" and can be appreciated anew here.It's not a flawless film, however. I personally hate ambiguous endings. I want to know who did it and why, and although it really is not necessary (real life killings oftentimes have no motive and the killer no history with his victim, after all) it still left me feeling irritated. I also had a serious problem suspending my belief when, at films end, the cops simply leave our heroine alone in the house to sleep her drugged sleep. True enough, they think they have caught the killer...but the girl is in shock, she's just committed a murder of her own (albeit, one of supposed self defense), and I'm supposed to accept it when they turn off the lights and just leave her there? What the hell? Okay, reshoot the last ten minutes or so, and I'll stop griping about the fact that we never find out who the killer is.I'm not sorry I watched it, if that's any consolation to you hopeful horror fans looking for some lost oldies but goodies. It's a good film. It could have been really great, but just fell a few inches short, that's all.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2003-07-01</date>
		<username>Krug Stillo (nhargii@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Classic Slasher Predating Halloween</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>SPOILERS AHEAD!!!After two competent attempts at horror (Children Shouldn't Play with Dead Things, 1972, and Deathdream, 1973), Bob Clarke made Black Christmas, one of the first American Slasher films that mixes the Italian giallo genre and the psycho?on-the-lose cycle initiated by Hitchcock's Psycho (1960). The results were original at the time, but even today, aware of all the clichés attributed to Slasher cinema, the film is still quite chilling.Set in an old dark sorority house during the festive season, a group of girls are brutally murdered by the raving lunatic that resides within the attic (Margot Kidder among them, who also starred in De Palma's Sisters, 1972, then found success as Lois Lane in Superman, 1978). The film even manages to mix horror and humour involving a buffoon excuse for a cop, Margot Kidder and John Saxon's detective character. The red herrings are characterised very well leading you to wonder whether it's Olivia Hussey's eccentric boyfriend (Keir Dullea of 2001: A Space Odyssey, 1968) or the ?escaped mental patient' that kidnapped and killed a young girl remains a mystery right up to the tense finale. During the film mechanics are used effectively, especially the now familiar ?caller is inside the house' routine. These are where the scares really lay. The foul-mouthed killer screams abuse at the girls down the telephone, occasionally snorting like a pig and mumbling something incoherent about a boy and girl called ?Angus and Billy'. The film ends with the fact that it is neither, but a deranged maniac who can only presumed to be Billy.The ending was intentionally left ambiguous for a planned sequel. It is even suggested that Bob Clarke, who then chose to do comedy, allowed John Carpenter to pick it up where he left it. As we all know what finally materialised wasn't Black Christmas 2, but the Daddy of the Slasher, Halloween (1978). This is one of the best Slashers, but definitely gets the award for the best psycho killer at Christmas, leading the way ahead of Silent Night, Deadly Night or Don't Open Until Christmas (1984).</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>An intelligent slasher?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>If you haven't seen this amazing classic horror film yet, do yourself a favor and see it. And remember, before you comment that it is cliche, keep in mind that this came out WAY before all the films that made certain elements of this cliche (killer being in the attic, dead cop outside, etc.). The thing that is very great about this film is that it's not strictly a horror film. There are well developed characters, plenty of comic relief, and some dramatic moments. The characters are not totally worthless and you don't want every one of them to die (except for the boyfriend who's name I can't recall right now, but he's a whiny ass... on purpose of course). The only stupid moronic moment in this is when the girl doesn't listen to the cop and goes upstairs. But they're sorority girls, right? But it's a quite well written script with good twists, a plot that keeps you entertained (unlike Friday the 13th, where you just sit and wait for another murder), knows how to be disturbing without being gory (there is no graphic violence but it's still quite violent and disturbing), and influenced an entire genre (while being better than any other film in that genre, with the possible exception of Maniac, Halloween, Texas Chainsaw Massacre if that counts as slasher, and a few others). I don't see how Halloween is THAT much of a rip-off of this. If you're cautious about this movie, thinking it's just going to be the same thing, it's not really. There's a few similar moments, but both films are excellent on their own. 8 1/2 / 10The best scene is the Christmas carol murder scene... brutal stuff! The worst is definitely where the girl goes upstairs when the cop tells her not to.</fullReview>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Forgotten gem</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A sorority house is menaced by a psychotic killer during the Christmas holidays. Time and countless imitations may have diminished the impact of this 1974 chiller, but "Black Christmas"--along with John Carpenter's "Halloween"--remains the textbook example of how to do a slasher flick right. Director Bob Clark, who went on to direct the much cheerier yuletide classic "A Christmas Story," always knows exactly where to place the camera to maximize suspense, and he uses shadow, music, and an old dark house to extraordinary effect. This forgotten gem also benefits greatly from three believable and likeable lead characters, nicely played by Olivia Hussey, Margot Kidder and Andrea Martin.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2002-09-20</date>
		<username>BillyBC</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Maybe the most underrated "slasher" movie of all time...?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>(****1/2 out of *****) This early mad-killer-in-the-house flick predates "Halloween" (as well as the similar, but much-inferior "When A Stranger Calls"), and it is seriously one of the scariest movies I have ever seen. A group of girls (including hotties Olivia Hussey and Margot Kidder and former "SCTV" regular Andrea Martin) staying in their sorority house over the Christmas break are plagued by bizarre, obscene phone calls from a schizophrenic maniac hiding up in the attic who begins knocking them off, one by one. Clark, famous mainly for directing "Porky's" and the much-beloved (and much more family-oriented) holiday movie "A Christmas Story", started his career directing horror movies (he also directed the psychedelic zombie flick "Children Shouldn't Play With Dead Things"), and he knows how to create an unbearably creepy atmosphere. He builds up so much tension and suspense towards the end of this one that it practically overwhelms you. The freaky phone calls and the disturbing open ending are enough to stay with you for days afterward. Clark and writer Roy Moore also manage to inject some much-needed humor into the oppressively gloomy script, provided mostly by Kidder's profane, heavy-drinking character, Barb. Art Hindle, Keir Dullea (`Dave?' from "2001") and horror movie veteran John Saxon also star in this sorely underrated thriller.HIGHLIGHT: The chilling moment of discovery when the heroine finds out that `the calls are coming from inside the house!' A highly influential horror-movie scene, oft-repeated, never bettered.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>vastly underrated? Yes! Fodder for Carpenter? A little bit!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>While I agree there are a very few instances in "Black Xmas" where the influence on Carpenter's "Halloween" can be implied, it's not, however a blue print for Carpenter's more influential(and more brilliant) work in "Halloween". Beyond the first few minutes and some camera work at the end,I find the comparisons coincidental and not easily attributable to either artist to begin with. It's also quite clear that these movies are not even attempting to accomplish the same goals. An example of a film whose tactics and story were clearly "lifted" from this tale would be "When a stranger calls".That having been said, "Black Christmas" IS a fine film. Clark succeeds in establishing a paranoid mood from the start with his very well done hand-held camera work, creepy understated score, clever lightning, and some fine performances. In short, "Black Christmas" is very worth seeing, but leave the comparisons to "Halloween" at the door they're really not as applicable as some would have you believe.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Small *SPOILERS* ahead. This is the Grandaddy of "Put down the phone and walk out of the house, now" films. If you really want a good scare then this is the one for you. Made in Canada, it pre-dates all those "phone call from a mad killer" movies and sets the standard which those films that followed really never matched. (The one exception being "Halloween"). The setting is a sorority house on a college campus at Christmas holidays. The terror and murder that is visited on those girls that are still left in the house is unrelenting. The story is basically a simple one.....horrible phone calls which result in horrible deaths. That voice on the phone will totally unnerve you...it lingers with you long after you have seen the film. Margot Kidder is perfect as a hard drinking, hard edged student and Olivia Hussey is truly beautiful as the gentle girl in love with Keir Dullea. In fact the whole cast, some of whom are unknown to American viewers, is quite good. Look for Andrea Martin from "Second City" as one of the sorority girls. The downbeat ending will leave you weak, as will the scenes with Hussey and Dullea when she rushes to the basement to escape. Why do people always go to the basement instead of leaving the house? If you like films that scare the bejeesus out of you, then don't miss this one. It is available on tape but don't watch it alone, and for God's sake, don't answer the phone!!!</fullReview>

I will say that this film is not only a great film, but one that possesses many of the slasher standards that are so predominant in those movies today, and it pre-dates almost all of them. While I won't say this film is the first horror movie to the killer P.O.V. shot, it is, along with Dario Argento's "Deep Red" (made the same year), one of the first to do it very, very effectively. And you'll see where countless others like, "When A Stranger Calls" and "House on Sorroity Row" lift many ideas, just to name a few.The director, Bob Clark, who is better known for his comedies of the 80's (Porky's, A Christmas Story) more than his horror movies of the 70's, is excellent in creating very human characters, a creepy atmosphere, and a very good story line, without generating terrible dialogue. And let's not forget the very scary killer. If you can find it on Ebay, I recommend one of his first horror films, "Deathdream" (a.k.a. "The Night Andy Came Home"). Very rare and very small budget, it is another example of why I wish Clark would have directed other horror films throughout his career. Also look for collaborations with writer/director Alan Ormsby on "Deranged" (1974) and "Children Shouldn't Play With Dead Things" (1972).See this movie. It is available on DVD. And thankfully, "Black Christmas 2" is finally in production. Grade A</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Must See</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm sure by now that everyone has heard how good this movie is, and they have heard right. This is one of the best early slasher films out there, if not THE best.The thing that makes this film so great is the fact that it came before them all and was the first slasher film to use all of the conventions we know so well. In the opening, we watch through the eyes of the killer as he circles a house where a group of sorority girls are having a party. We heard his heavy breathing as he climbs up the trellis of the house and enters through the attic, events we have seen countless times afterward. Thus begins the film, which captivated me from that one scene and didn't make me lose my interest until the powerful finale. From the first phone call immediately following that scene, to the killer's heavy voice at the end as a shot of the house is shown, this is a stylish and suspenseful film. As usual, lovely Olivia Hussey delivers a wonderful performance and so does Margot Kidder as a foul mouthed and drunken sorority sister. One by one the sisters are stalked by the psychopath and he could be just about anyone. Hussey even begins to suspect that her boyfriend has something to do with the murders, but as the audience knows, the suspects never end up being killers. Full of weird and frightening phone calls, some great performances and clever dialogue, this is thrilling and truly original horror film, one of the first of it's kind, which makes it better than ever. This has been an obvious inspiration for numerous slashers out there, including Halloween, which was originally planned as a sequel to this film. This is a masterpiece of tension and a truly horrifying experience. The unconventional ending is also a plus and a very unexpected way to end the film. Turn off the lights, grab a bowl of popcorn and enjoy. This is a definite must see...</fullReview>

		<username>Kelper99</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Simply one of the best -- and most underrated -- horror movies ever.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A brilliant work of terror and suspense which predates Halloween. It's the simplest of stories: a psychopath holes away in a sorority house attic and makes shockingly obscene and bizarre phonecalls to the unwitting housemates, who he proceeds to knock off one by one. Sound like old hat? SEE THE MOVIE. This film understands that what we HEAR is scarier than what we SEE -- and what is hidden is more terrifying than what is revealed. The sick phone calls, in which the killer assumes about a half dozen personalities (crying baby, nagging mother, twisted sexual deviant, scared little boy, abusive father, calm killer) compose the most frightening soundtrack since The Exorcist. No other horror movie since has utilized sound so effectively. Only, perhaps, The Blair Witch Project, with its use of a baby crying, demonstrated at least a partial understanding of how a single simple poignant sound effect is all you need to scare the silly putty out of the audience. But Blair Witch is nothin' compared to "Black Christmas." This movie was originally called "Stranger in the House" when I saw it as a kid. But you can now buy the special edition DVD under the title "Black Christmas." It's a must see.</fullReview>

		<date>2001-03-13</date>
		<username>Glyde Stick</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Why can't they make films like this anymore?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Having read several of the comments regarding this film and how it was so influential on many slasher films made since, I was expecting an outstanding thriller. I must say I was not disappointed. This was an actual scary movie! The refreshing thing about this movie was it did not use extremely gory special effects or cheap sex scenes (although sexual innuendos are littered throughout the film).Aside from being a little dated (the 70's fashion was rampant) this film has really held up over time. Having seen most (if not all) of the films referred to in the previous comments as having "ripped off" Black Christmas, it is obvious this film was indeed used as a blueprint for several of the "slasher" movies that followed.*** The following commentary contains SPOILERS ***Having said that I do disagree, however, with several comments made that John Carpenter's "Halloween" was a blatant ripoff of this movie. Aside from a homicidal killer who murders girls during a holiday, I did not see a lot of similarities. Granted the victims were killed in similar methods (stabbing or strangulation) but let's be honest - except in the numerous Friday the 13th movies where each sequel featured new and exciting ways to kill people (everything from hedge clippers to spear guns), there aren't that many ways a psychopath uses to kill someone in a slasher film (and stabbing and strangulation are pretty common). But I didn't see Michael Myers playing with any of the victims long after they were dead (as the killer in this film did with Claire, the first victim, who he rocked in a chair from time to time). I don't recall Michael Myers making repeated and lengthy obscene phone calls to his victims before he killed them. In Halloween, there was no question of who the killer was at the end. I didn't see the killer in Black Christmas wear a mask, steal his dead sister's headstone, or escape from an insane asylum. So where, exactly, does the "blatant ripoff" occur?Slasher films all have a basic premise (psycho killer for various reasons stalk and kill helpless victims who may or may not have been a part of the killer's past) so you can pick any two films out of the slasher genre and draw similarities. Granted "Black Christmas" was one of the first films in this genre and, as I mentioned earlier, there are remarkable resemblances between this film and others (most notably the opening scene of "When a Stranger Calls"). But I would hesitate to say any one slasher film was a "blatant ripoff" of another. Even the original "Friday the 13th" had enough differences from "Halloween" to avoid the "blatant ripoff" stigma.The Bottom Line: Black Christmas is a very good and effective thriller with an undeniable influence on the entire slasher genre that followed it. I would highly recommend it to anyone who enjoys a good tingle of the spine.</fullReview>
	</review>

	<review>
		<date>1999-10-06</date>
		<username>Darragh O' Donoghue (hitch1899_@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A creepy tonic to Christmas cheer.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>An excellent horror film, which manages to build up a quiet, considerable power. While it may not be out-and-out BOO! YIKES! scary (very little is these days), it combines narrative and subtext to an agreeably creepy pitch. Imagine an episode of THE X-FILES without the gloss, or alien nonsense, or enervating conspiracy theories, or even Mulder and Scully. Even the amusing accents (the film is Canadian) cease to grate after a while.The story is set in a placid all-girl sorority house on campus in some Canadian university, at Christmas time. The girls are having a sedate party with some boy friends. Outside a breathing camera lurches towards the house... Like Argento's SUSPIRIA, this all-female community is the site of the most horrific repressions, anxieties, fears, waiting to burst out, both within (figured as alcoholism, unwanted pregnancy) and without (rapes, murders, obscene phone calls). Cleverly, though, the girls don't go hysterically loopy - everything remains externalised - much more chilling, even if it lacks the full on psychogynaecology of the Italian masterpiece.For example, one girl's mother is clearly indifferent to her, gold-digging, she claims, after other men. Another is a virgin who has previously, we deduce, lived in a very repressive, rigid family atmosphere. Indeed, one of the noteworthy things about BLACK is how these girls are away from home for the first time - they have cut away from authority, patriarchy and tradition. The film is not necessarily a reactionary plea, however - the girls' substitute authority figures - boyfriends - become the locus of the terror; and although their self-made family verges on the parodic (with their seemingly jovial, frustrated alcoholic housekeeper as mother substitute), it is a genuine, if finally useless, structure of support.I suppose the film could be seen as an examination of the deep traumas buried underneath the vociferous self-confidence of contemporary feminism - after all, it was directed by the man who would make that radical treatise on gender relations, PORKY'S. The main plot centres on the lead's right to have an abortion, and the terrifying phone calls - a horrifying, babbling cacophony from the beyond - seem like the pleas of a dying child, or the malicious threats of an Id-mother. The idea of the disembodied or unreferential voice (those things denied a presence in the film, and maybe our society; e.g. the psychological trauma of abortion), miscommunication, misread signs, disinformation all suggest a culture where all recognisable meaning has gone haywire. As this is the film's anxiety, we may feel that it is somewhat right-wing.On the other hand, it's thematic interest is the family, set in a family away from a 'real' family. The BLACK of the title in relation to CHRISTMAS suggests diabolical overtones, a distorted mirror of the Holy Family celebrated at this time. Maybe the film is reactionary - charging newly independent women with destroying the structures of family and tradition, and unloosing untold terrors and demons.Or maybe it's very sympathetic to the characters' plight. Patriarchy in all its forms pervade the film - the unseen killer/rapist; the ineffectual police lieutenant; the self-obsessed boyfriends; the downright idiotic cop. The only clever, likable characters are the women. And, as in the case of Barb, their rejection of family is not necessarily willed. The path to independence is certainly not made easy in this culture.The great thing about BLACK is how it shows the sheer ineffectuality of trying to specify the unspecifiable. It is obviously madness to think that Peter could have committed all these crimes, and yet it is quite reasonable to demonise him - he reveals the monster beneath the sensitive New Man. The heroine discovers that she can rely on no-one but herself. Even this isn't enough. Notwithstanding the unreassuring ending, she still has to deal with the fallout of her actions, which are by no means resolved. There is still the issue of the abortion, which is one reason the demon is not dead. There were obscene phone calls and rapes before she knew she was pregnant: the fears may be subjective, but the inability to connect everything makes the film the more scarily effective.As a horror film, BLACK's excellence derives from the disparity between the hysteria of the subject matter, and the cool film style, suggestive, sinister, yet withdrawn. The breathy point of view camerawork may seem tiresome after HALLOWEEN and its dozy imitators, but here the idea of something submerged, concealed, unknowable, unconquerable, not even human, never mind the cartoon slasher we're used to, is superbly realised, slowing down time. The images of the first dead girl, with the doll baby in her lap, and the cat purring beside her, will haunt me for a long, long time.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>I watched this movie after much searching through Mom &amp; Pop video stores and found a pristine copy. It scared my pants off! BLACK CHRISTMAS is one of those movies that is an unsung classic and is most appreciated by horror buffs. I tried to get my friends to watch it, but they are all into gory, graphic horror opuses like SCREAM and URBAN LEGEND. Unfortunately, those movies have very few scares and are just cheap rip-offs posing as tributes to great films like this one. The gore in slasher movies is what ruined the entire horror genre. BLACK CHRISTMAS, HALLOWEEN, TEXAS CHAINSAW MASSACRE, the older films rely on suspense, mind-numbing suspense and require your imagination. People are spoiled by the gore effects in films now, sometimes the effects are gratuitous and don't move the story along at all! BLACK CHRISTMAS' story moves along briskly, dragging the viewer into it, making the movie seem so real! Horror buffs should appreciate the fact that BLACK CHRISTMAS attempts to "befriend" its audience instead of "ripping its guts out"!</fullReview>

		<date>1999-07-02</date>
		<username>Anton</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The first and best slasher flick.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>You may argue that Psycho was the first and best slasher flick, but you'd be wrong. Psycho's events took place in an isolated area, and so it is disqualified from the slasher genre, however, Black Christmas was the first film to bring terror to suburbia. Girls in a sorority house (including Romeo and Juliet's Olivia Hussey and Superman's Margot Kidder) are victimised by a prank caller, and then those within their number start to go missing. Definitely the scariest parts of the film are the parts where the police make Olivia Hussey listen to the demented ramblings of the phantom caller. Although John Carpenter's Halloween was acclaimed as the first slasher pic, this film was made four years before Halloween, and contains many of the elements that seem so 'original' in Halloween (such as the killer's first person perspective, and its almost omniscient characteristics). It is a crying shame that this movie never got the success (nor the critical acclaim) that is so well deserves. It certainly ought to go down in history as the definitive stalk 'n' slash.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Underrated classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Many offer Halloween as the classic against which all stalker movies should be compared, but four years earlier, Black Christmas, a minor but exemplary effort succeeded in being far creepier, and avoided many of the former's failings. The telephone scenes are unnerving, the killer truly scary, and the ending is superlative, with a terrifying coda. It strains credulity sometimes, but the terrific use of setting, especially the snowbound Canadian campus, and the admirable character development (so it's not Anna Karenina; how many horror films actually give attention to the minor characters?) make for a highly recommended, as well as historically important, terror film. Olivia Hussey gives a standout performance, Margot Kidder steals her scenes, and Andrea Martin makes an impact in a non-comedic role. Definitely outshines its knockoffs.</fullReview>
